3D Printing of Bioceramics for Bone

3D Printing of Bioceramics for Bone Tissue Engineering 

Abstract: Bioceramics have frequent use in functional restoration of hard tissues to improve human well-being. Additive manufacturing (AM) also known as 3D printing is an innovative material processing technique extensively applied to produce bioceramic parts or scaolds in a layered perspicacious manner. Moreover, the applications of additive manufacturing in bioceramics have the capability to reliably fabricate the commercialized scaolds tailored for practical clinical applications, and the potential to survive in the new era of eective hard tissue fabrication. The similarity of the materials with human bone histomorphometry makes them conducive to use in hard tissue engineering scheme. The key objective of this manuscript is to explore the applications of bioceramics-based AM in bone tissue engineering. Furthermore, the article comprehensively and categorically summarizes some novel bioceramics based AM techniques for the restoration of bones. At prior stages of this article, dierent ceramics processing AM techniques have been categorized, subsequently, processing of frequently used materials for bone implants and complexities associated with these materials have been elaborated. At the end, some novel applications of bioceramics in orthopedic implants and some future directions are also highlighted to explore it further. This review article will help the new researchers to understand the basic mechanism and current challenges in neophyte techniques and the applications of bioceramics in the orthopedic prosthesis.

Keywords: bioceramics; additive manufacturing; scaolds; bone tissue engineering

1. Introduction

Additive manufacturing or 3D printing has got attention in scaold design and manufacturing for tissue engineering applications. Initially, this technique was developed by Sachs et al., to create the ink-jet freestyle printing towards the latter part of the 20th century [1]. Later on, it was extended in tailoring the perfect scaolds on its user-friendly capabilities, which considered the transformation of computer aided design (CAD) information to a rapid and reliable production line of constructs with the coveted material, porosity, and measurements [2,3]. Moreover, it showed a time and cost-ecient potential coupled with interconnected structures, specifically hard tissue deformity regeneration.

Recently, clinical preliminaries and contextual analyses revealed its resounding accomplishments in the field of orthopedic bioengineering. While this procedure has shown significant potential, specific diculties tend to enhance patient-particular scaolds for standard acknowledgment in regenerative medicine [4‚Äì6]. During the past few decades, many advanced biomaterials were introduced in the biomedical field including dierent ceramic materials for the skeletal repair and reconstruction. These materials in the field of medical implants are often referred to as ‚Äúbioceramics‚Äù [7]. Bioceramics are peculiar in nature due to their exceptional biological and osteoinduction properties. These materials are specific for scaolds due to capability to create propagation, self-adhesion, distinction and bone

Materials

2019, 12, 3361; doi:10.3390/ma12203361 www.mdpi.com/journal/materials

Materials 2019, 12, 3361 2 of 26 tissues regeneration [8]. Furthermore, excellent chemical and mechanical properties such as better osteoconductivity, superior wear resistant and biocompatibility enabled them as a substitute for bone restoration, [9,10]. It can anticipate that bioceramics have a future due to increasing bone replacement operation per year due to increasing aging population [11].

The clinical importance of AM ceramic scaold design and implantation envelops an invaluable method for quick and reliable production of hard tissue substitution replica of the biological context of natural bone [12]. In view of the way that customized scaold can be prepared that suits an individual patient‚Äôs skeletal imperfection, layer-by-layer sintering is regarded as a lucrative discipline for the utilization of ceramic-based bone substitutes in regenerative medicine [13]. Besides, utilizing AM ceramic scaolds as medication conveyance systems, it is becoming more attractive and relevant to the bioengineering environment [14‚Äì16].

This article is divided into six sections; Section 1 details the bioceramics potential, Section 2 oers an overview of the AM techniques used to fabricate ceramic parts. Section 3 presents achievements in the production of hydroxyapatite (HA); Section 4 depicts about tricalcium phosphate (TCP) and Section 5 describes about bioactive glass (BG) using dierent AM techniques. Section 6 concludes some important findings with some current challenges and future opportunities in this field.

2. Additive Manufacturing Technologies to Produce Ceramic Parts

Additive manufacturing has been classified into two major classes such as acellular and cellular techniques for biomaterials. The cellular category includes the printing of live cells, while the acellular category does not consider any type of live cells in printing. 2. Additive Manufacturing Technologies to Produce Ceramic parts

Additive manufacturing has been classified into two major classes such as acellular and cellular techniques for biomaterials. The cellular category includes the printing of live cells, while the acellular category does not consider any type of live cells in printing. Figure 1 shows different acellular AM techniques for biomaterials that have been classified as per recommendations of American Society for Testing of Materials (ASTM) [17]. The major AM techniques employed in the processing of bioceramics have been discussed in the following section. Figure 1. Different types of acellular techniques for biomaterials additive manufacturing (AM).

2.1. Binder Jetting

Binder jetting technique was developed in the early 1990s at Massachusetts Institute of Technology (MIT) [18]. Figure 2 depicts the schematic of binder jetting. In this technique, the binder is selectively used from powder bed to create 3D objects. Binder jetting is a valuable technique for printing powder materials [19,20]. The particle size of the powder has a key influence on powder flowability in binder jetting.

erent types of acellular techniques for biomaterials additive manufacturing (AM).

2.1.

Binder

Jetting

Binder jetting technique was developed in the early 1990s at Massachusetts Institute of Technology (MIT) [18]. Figure 2 depicts the schematic of binder jetting. In this technique, the binder is selectively used from powder bed to create 3D objects. Binder jetting is a valuable technique for printing powder materials [19,20]. The particle size of the powder has a key influence on powder flowability in binder jetting. Materials 2019, 12, 3361 3 of 26 Materials 2019, 12, x FOR PEER REVIEW 3 of 29 Figure 2. Schematic diagram of binder jetting mechanism [18]. For dry binder jetting, large size particles are preferred due to its outstanding flowability and less surface area. The powder size not only affects the flowability but also significantly affects the quality of the final product. Numerous researchers have reported less surface roughness using fine powder in the binder jetting. The effect of the powder shape is less, as compared to the powder size. However, spherical shape powders have better flowability and lesser friction compared to faceted powders [21‚Äì23].

2.2. Direct Energy Deposition (DED)

Direct energy deposition-based AM techniques uses energy into a small region to simultaneously deposit, melt and solidify the material such as wire or powder [24]. The direct energy source can be electrical, or laser beam can be used to melt the metal, ceramics or composite materials. Laser assisted Direct Deposition techniques such as laser cladding, laser engineered net shaping (LENS‚Ñ¢), and laser melt injection are common examples of this technique. In ceramic Direct Energy Deposition (DED), the printing head of the apparatus contains a nozzle that feeds ceramic powder particles to the focal point of the laser beam. The ceramic powder melts and solidifies in layer-wise fashion on a substrate [25]. Figure 3 is the schematic illustration of LENS [26]. Figure 2. Schematic diagram of binder jetting mechanism [18].

For dry binder jetting, large size particles are preferred due to its outstanding flowability and less surface area. The powder size not only aects the flowability but also significantly aects the quality of the final product. Numerous researchers have reported less surface roughness using fine powder in the binder jetting. The eect of the powder shape is less, as compared to the powder size. However, spherical shape powders have better flowability and lesser friction compared to faceted powders [21‚Äì23].

2.2.

Direct Energy Deposition (DED)

Direct energy deposition-based AM techniques uses energy into a small region to simultaneously deposit, melt and solidify the material such as wire or powder [24]. The direct energy source can be electrical, or laser beam can be used to melt the metal, ceramics or composite materials. Laser assisted Direct Deposition techniques such as laser cladding, laser engineered net shaping (LENS¬ó), and laser melt injection are common examples of this technique.

In ceramic Direct Energy Deposition (DED), the printing head of the apparatus contains a nozzle that feeds ceramic powder particles to the focal point of the laser beam. The ceramic powder melts and solidifies in layer-wise fashion on a substrate [25]. Figure 3 is the schematic illustration of LENS [26].

The major advantages of DED are better compatibility with a wide range of biomaterial viscosities, higher resolution and greater cell density that provide higher control of cell-to-cell adhesions [27]. Besides these advantages, DED has many challenges such as, low speed, cost, high complexity and limited capability to manufacture heterogeneous tissue parts [28]. Due to these challenges, the usage of DED is very limited as compared to other AM techniques particularly in bone tissue engineering. The DED technology needs more research to enhance its productivity.

The DED technology needs more research to enhance its productivity.

2.3. Material Extrusion and Jetting

Extrusion assisted additive manufacturing deposits a continuous layer by layer deposition of ceramic loaded paste to create 3D objects. Various terms are used to refer to this technology for instance, Fused Deposition of Ceramics (FDC), Robocasting, Extrusion Freeform Fabrication (EFF), Direct Ink Writing (DIW), Slurry Deposition, and Dispense Plotting [29]. In Fused Deposition, dense ceramic particles (up to 60 vol%) are spread into a wax or thermoplastic filament after which the flexible filament is partly melted and extruded from a moving deposition head onto a fixed worktable layer-by-layer. However, in robocasting, ceramic slurry is ejected from a precise nozzle to form a filament that is directly deposited in a designed pattern to create complex 3D objects in a layer-by-layer fashion [30]. In another research work, an indirect Fused Deposition Modeling (FDM) method was applied to prepare ceramic parts. At the preliminary stage, FDM was used to prepare a honeycomb shaped polymer structural mold. Secondly, the ceramic slurry was permeated into the polymer mold-sintering to remove the mold. The porous ceramics made a correct pore size and porosity through this technique [31]. Another technique named Extrusion-based bioprinting has also a greater potential in perspective of deposition and printing speed compared to other AM techniques. This technique is also beneficial to achieve better scalability in a shorter time [32], wide range flexibility of bioinks selection [33]. This is because developing new bioinks is a critical procedure for quick, sustainable and safe delivery of cells in a biomimetic microenvironment [32]. Besides many advantages, some complexities are also associated with this technology such as low resolution and shear stress effect on cells. The schematic of the process with part microstructure was shown in Figure 4. Figure 3. Typical Schematic representation of laser engineered net shaping (LENS) [26].

2.4. Powder Bed Fusion

Powder bed fusion (PBF) technologies are among pioneer commercially used AM techniques created by the University of Texas USA. Selective laser sintering (SLS) based PBF technique [18], which melts the ceramic powder by laser energy source. The laser sintered the powder nearly to the melting point of the material to make each layer according to the given 3D design. The laser beam scans each new single layer of free-packed powder particles and consolidates them by sintering this process and proceeds in a layer-wise fashion to complete the final 3D object [35‚Äì37]. SLS is a powder bed fusion process have numerous applications in the bioengineering field such as to prepare customized products, biomedical implants as well as orthopedic implants [38]. The major disadvantage of SLS is the usage of higher temperatures that limits the insertion of biomaterial and cells into SLS scaffolds during the manufacturing process [39]. A schematic diagram illustrates the underlying operating system [40] of the powder bed fusion provided in Figure 6. While SLS technology is amended, the PBF method to increase machine efficiency. Figure 5. Schematic diagram depicts the basic working principle of ink-jet 3D printing [34].

2.5. Vat Polymerization (SLA)

Vat Polymerization also known as stereolithography (SLA) is a promising AM technique to fabricate tissue scaffolds in the field of regenerative medicine. The SLA technique has exceptional control over porosity of scaffolds, pore sizes, design flexibility, and interconnectivity [41]. Despite excellent advantages, numerous researchers have highlighted several challenges in scaffold manufacturing such as, difficult in creating micron-sized scaffold due to over curing and layer thickness. In addition, some of the frequently used biomaterials in bone tissue engineering have shown compatibility with SLA due to limitations in viscosity, refractive index and stability [42]. Some other problems such as some SLA processes light pixels restrict in-plane microstructure construction. Although indirect SLA have overcome this problem, it is a costly, time and material consuming process [43]. Li et al. have used indirect stereolithography to manufacture microporous Œ≤-TCP. The resin molds were prepared through this technique and filled with filled with aqueous thermosetting ceramic suspension for ceramic gel casting. The heat treatment process was used to remove the molds. Results have concluded that TCP scaffolds after sintering have shown desired porosity, shape and higher strength were obtained [44]. Some other researchers have mentioned preparation of 3D objects by photo-curing a liquid resin through ultraviolet (UV) laser in a layerwise fashion [45,46]. The major advantage of this process includes better surface finish and accuracy [47]. A schematic of three different light sources used in stereolithography provided in Figure 7 [18]. Table 1 summarizing some basic bioprinting techniques for bone tissue engineering. Figure 6. Schematic showing the working principle of powder bed fusion technique [40].

3. Additive Manufacturing of Bioceramics

In the last few decades, bioceramics have frequently been used in the restoration and replacements of injured tissues due to numerous advantages such as precise chemical composition, which has a vital role in the integration of hard and soft tissues [48,49]. Hydroxyapatite (HA) Ca10(PO4) 6(OH)2 is one bioceramic to have frequently been employed as a scaold material for bone tissue engineering owing to its exceptional biocompatibility and resemblance to natural bone material [50‚Äì52]. It is often combined with a biopolymer or bioceramics to enhance binding interaction and mechanical properties of the material during the AM process [53,54].

Beta tri-calcium phosphate (

-TCP) is a suitable material for craniofacial defects owing to its excellent biodegradability, wear resistance and chemical bonding with the bone tissues under all load bearing conditions [55,56]. The critical challenge for -TCP is to maintain the sintering temperature of 1100 C. Above this temperature, beta tri-calcium phosphate (-TCP) transforms to alpha tri-calcium phosphate (-TCP) that is soluble and chemically unstable, as compared to -TCP [57,58]. In addition, bioglasses are also extensively used in hard tissue implants due to their excellent bonding capability with hard and soft both tissues. Bioglasess are also extremely helpful in upregulating the osteogenesis, however, their application in load bearing bone defects are very limited due to their high brittleness, low fracture toughness and mechanical strength [59‚Äì61]. Properties of some frequently used ceramic material for bone tissue engineering illustrated in Table 2.

Table 2. A brief review of ceramic materials and its properties used in 3D printing of Scaolds. ecting the performance of bioceramics is Ca to P ratio that aects the dissolution property. Calcium phosphates with lower Ca to P ratio (-TCP) have higher solubility and acidic nature as compared to calcium-phosphate having high Ca to P ratio (HA) [62]. Table 3 shows that lower the Ca/P ratio higher the CaP dissolution [63]. 3.1. Hydroxyapatite (HA)

Hydroxyapatite (HA) portrayed as CaÙÄ¨µÙÄ¨¥(POÙÄ¨∏) ÙÄ¨∫(OH)ÙÄ¨∂, encompasses almost 65% of the entire bone mass. It is less toxic and more stable, as compared to other calcium-phosphate due to their desirable Ca to P ratio of 1.67. The hydroxyapatite has major inorganic part of human bone and teeth to develop the properties and novel applications of bioceramics for hard tissue replacements [65‚Äì68]. Numerous researchers have reported HA scaffolds in the bone and teeth transplants [69‚Äì77]. Laser Stereolithography has been identified as one of the most effective and frequently used AM techniques to produce complex HA parts. Barry et al. have prepared HA-based oligocarbonate dimethacrylate (OCM-2) composite scaffolds using helium-cadmium (HeCd) based laser technology. The outcomes referred the laser-based HA scaffolds provided fortified cell attachment inside the scaffold. Through laser machining, toxic leftovers were removed effectively through supercritical carbon dioxide (scCO2) to make scaffolds biocompatible. The HA based composite materials treated by scCO2 showed better attachment of cells in both vivo and vitro studies [78]. In a very recent study, a bio-ink was prepared for 3D printing by dispersing two different types of hydroxyapatites, nano hydroxyapatite (nHA) and deproteinized bovine bone (DBB) into collagen. Thereby, a porous structure was created by 3D printing. The chemical and physical properties of the materials, including biocompatibility and effect on the osteogenic differentiation of the human bone marrow-derived mesenchyme stem cells (hBMSCs) were investigated. Both nHA/CoL and DBB/CoL Bio-inks were used to print biomimic 3D scaffolds effectively. The outcomes from this study showed that the two types of hydroxyapatite composites which help hBMSCs proliferation and differentiation proved to be a promising candidate for a 3D scaffold bio-ink [79]. Woeszn et al. fabricated microporous HA scaffolds having a pore size of 450 Œºm through stereolithography coupled with ceramic gelcating. A photosensitive liquid resin filled with water based thermosetting slurry was used in the mold. The mold resin and sintering were burnt to achieve Figure 8. Schematic representation of bone tissue engineering [64].

3.1.

Hydroxyapatite (HA)

Hydroxyapatite (HA) portrayed as Ca10(PO4) 6(OH)2, encompasses almost 65% of the entire bone mass. It is less toxic and more stable, as compared to other calcium-phosphate due to their desirable Ca to P ratio of 1.67. The hydroxyapatite has major inorganic part of human bone and teeth to develop the properties and novel applications of bioceramics for hard tissue replacements [65‚Äì68]. Numerous researchers have reported HA scaolds in the bone and teeth transplants [69‚Äì77].

Laser Stereolithography has been identified as one of the most eective and frequently used

AM techniques to produce complex HA parts. Barry et al. have prepared HA-based oligocarbonate dimethacrylate (OCM-2) composite scaolds using helium-cadmium (HeCd) based laser technology. The outcomes referred the laser-based HA scaolds provided fortified cell attachment inside the scaold. Through laser machining, toxic leftovers were removed eectively through supercritical carbon dioxide (scCO2) to make scaolds biocompatible. The HA based composite materials treated by scCO2 showed better attachment of cells in both vivo and vitro studies [78]. In a very recent study, a bio-ink was prepared for 3D printing by dispersing two dierent types of hydroxyapatites, nano hydroxyapatite (nHA) and deproteinized bovine bone (DBB) into collagen. Thereby, a porous structure was created by 3D printing. The chemical and physical properties of the materials, including biocompatibility and eect on the osteogenic dierentiation of the human bone marrow-derived mesenchyme stem cells (hBMSCs) were investigated. Both nHA/CoL and DBB/CoL Bio-inks were used to print biomimic 3D scaolds eectively. The outcomes from this study showed that the two types of hydroxyapatite composites which help hBMSCs proliferation and dierentiation proved to be a promising candidate for a 3D scaold bio-ink [79]. Woeszn et al. fabricated microporous HA scaolds having a pore size of 450 m through stereolithography coupled with ceramic gelcating. A photosensitive liquid resin filled with water based thermosetting slurry was used in the mold. The mold resin and sintering were burnt to achieve the desired features. The final Scaolds were seeded on MC3T3-E1 cells for 14 days under deep penetration of cells to achieve outstanding osteogenesis as shown in Figure 9 [80].

The AM based extrusion process is also very common to manufacture HA scaffolds. The robocasting based extrusion process contains ceramic ink in the form of water-based viscous slurry deposited on a robotic nozzle in layer-wise fashion based on computer-aided design. The process contains high loading of HA particles to minimize the cracks and distortion during sintering. Saiz et al. have fabricated HA scaffolds with controlled pore sizes through robocasting extrusion to find the optimum sintering temperature. The hydroxyapatite slurry was prepared by mixing 40‚Äì50 vol.% of HA powder in distilled water, 1.5 wt% of Darvan C dispersant, (~7 mg/mL of solution) hydroxypropyl methylcellulose, an adequate amoqunt of polyethyleneimine (PEI) and at the end HNOÙÄ¨∑ or HNÙÄ¨∏OH to balance the pH level of the slurry. Results concluded that porous HA scaffolds manufactured with robocasting showed the sintering temperature should remain between 1100 ¬∞C ~ 1200 ¬∞C and no phase change was observed for firing 1300 ¬∞C for 3 h. The characteristics of printed scaffolds through this technique have been presented in Figure 10 [81]. Figure 9. 3.2. Tricalcium Phosphate (TCP)

Since the last two decades, beta tri-calcium phosphate (Œ≤-TCP) ceramic-based scaffolds have clinically accepted the bone graft replacement materials in several orthopedic and dental applications [83‚Äì89]. Immunoregulation capability was tested on macrophages and rat bone marrow stromal cells of the coated scaffold was used to test osteogenic capability. The scaffold exhibit improved osteogenesis, better cell compatibility and also helpful to regulate the immune response as compared to traditionally manufactured scaffold. 3.2.

Tricalcium Phosphate (TCP)

Since the last two decades, beta tri-calcium phosphate (-TCP) ceramic-based scaolds have clinically accepted the bone graft replacement materials in several orthopedic and dental applications [83‚Äì89]. The TCP contains , 0 and 0 phases and Ca to P ratio is about 1.5. Cao et al. manufactured sphingosine 1-phosphate (S1P) coated

-TCP scaold. Immunoregulation capability was tested on macrophages and rat bone marrow stromal cells of the coated scaold was used to test osteogenic capability. The scaold exhibit improved osteogenesis, better cell compatibility and also helpful to regulate the immune response as compared to traditionally manufactured scaold. Figure 11 is the representation of 3D printed scaold and its cell viability [90].

Bian et al. introduced a novel stereolithographic method to produce osteochondral beta-tricalcium phosphate/collagen scaold. This bio-inspired scaold manufactured by a combination of ceramic stereolithography (CSL) and gel casting using (-TCP) and type-I collagen. Histological examination was performed to investigate the morphological properties between cartilage and bone. The obtained information from this examination were used to design biomimetic biphasic scaolds. The pores size of -TCP scaolds varied between 700‚Äì900 m with 50‚Äì65% porosity and compressive strength of 12 MPa. Physical locking formed by biomimetic transitional structure was used to achieve an adequate binding force among cartilage phase and a ceramic phase. The results concluded that CSL performed well in comparison with traditional techniques to get an ideal scaold for bone tissue engineering applications [91]. 3.3.

Bioactive Glass (BG)

Bioactive glasses (BG) are the type of bioceramics that exist in both nonporous and solid forms. The bio glass contains silicon dioxide, sodium oxide, calcium oxide and phosphorous. Dierent types of bioglass have been created by varying the vol.% of these components [94]. Silicate part plays have an essential role in the biocompatibility of bioactive glasses. The bio glass with 45‚Äì52 vol.% silicate has ideal bone-graft bonding [95]. The 45S5-bioglass is a well-known commercially available extensively used in bone replacement [96]. However, bioglasses have also some limitations due to poor mechanical properties and brittleness that makes them unsuitable for load-bearing applications, internally brittle and deficient mechanical strength. However, several researchers have reported dierent additives, such as metal, polymer and ceramic to enhance the mechanical properties [97‚Äì101].

Recently, Nommeots-Nomn et al. robocasted bioglass scaolds with a 150 m interconnected pore size (41‚Äì43% porosity) and measured compressive strengths were 32‚Äì48 MPa. The network connectivity

(NC) of these scaolds is like the 45S5 bioglass. In this process, ICIE16 and PSrBG compositions were used comprising <

50 mol% SiO2 to maintain the amorphous structure and to achieve the required NC closer to 45S5 bioglass. The manufactured scaolds were compared with 13‚Äì93 vol.% composition bioglass. The comparison highlighted that 3D porous scaolds have similar NC values with 45S5 bioglass using two low silica contents. In addition, Pluronic F-127 binder could be accepted as a universal binder for bioactive glasses regardless of their composition and reactivity. Results also showed that ICIE16 and PSrBG based scaolds are highly reactive and significantly enhanced the bone regeneration speed [102]. Padilla et al. used calcined bioglass suspension to fabricate porous scaolds through integrating the stereolithography and gel-casting method. A polymeric negative mold was used via stereolithography to cast bioglass suspension with Darvan-811 (sodium polyacrylate) as a dispersant. The slurry containing 50 vol% was heated at 1100 C for 55 s and later it was polymerized. The negative mold was removed by heat treatment. The scaolds containing interconnected 3D channels of 400‚Äì470 m length and 1.4 m of pore diameter. The results illustrated that the entire interconnected porous scaold was created by this method [103].

Westhauser et al. inspected the osteo-inductive properties of dierent polymer coated 3D-45S5 bioglass scaolds. These scaolds are seeded with human mesenchymal stem cells (hMSC) implanted into immunodeficient mice. The gelatin, cross-linked gelatin, and poly (3-hydroxybutyrate-co-3hydroxyvalerate) type coatings were used. histomorphometry and micro-computed topography analysis were performed to evaluate the new formation after eight weeks of implantation. Although, every bioglass scaolds showed noticeable bone regeneration. However, gelatin-coated bioglass scaolds showed highest cell formation in comparison with other coated-bioglasses, as shown in Figure 13 [104]. 4. Application of Bioceramics in Orthopedic Implants

Natural bone has self-repair capability after the damage. The smaller fractures heal itself correctly, however segmental bone defects (SBDs) lead to permanent paralysis [109,110]. SBDs fractures treated with autologous bone graft technique requires harvesting of non-vital bone, such as, the iliac crest.

However, some complexities are also associated with bone grafting such as bone availability, the mismatch between harvested bone and aected site, morbidity of donor site results in poor integration [111]. Over the past three decades, a variety of synthetic materials have been introduced to overcome the complexities such as calcium phosphates (bioactive glasses) and hybrid bioceramics-polymer materials [112‚Äì115]. Roohani-Esfahani et al. fabricated glass-ceramic scaolds with hexagonal pore structure via extrusion-based AM method shown in Figure 14. The fabricated scaolds have 150 times greater strength compared to polymeric-composite scaolds and five times greater than ceramic-glass scaolds having same porosity. The study has shown that these scaolds have excellent capability to load-bearing and segmental bone defects treatment [125].

Fierz et al. prepared HA based cylindrical scaolds ranging from nanometer to millimeter with straight channels and micro-pores through n-HA granules, ink-jet 3D printing AM technique. The structure of 3D-printed scaolds is almost similar to human cortical and cancellous bone. The histological analysis has confirmed that osteogenic-stimulated progenitor-based 3D-printed scaolds are suitable for clinical use [126].

In another study, a robocasting technique was utilized to transport bone morphogenic protein 2 (BMP-2). HA slurry and polymethylmethacrylate (PMMA) microspheres were mixed together to achieve controlled microporosity. Resins were eliminated by sintering the scaolds at 1300 C for 2 h. Thereafter, 10 g of bone morphogenic protein 2 was added to the microporous scaolds in goat bone for in vivo characterization for 4 and 8 weeks. Outcomes from this study showed great potential for manufacturing HA scaolds containing interconnected porosity. Furthermore, the existence of bone morphogenic protein 2 and micro porosity upgraded scaold osteogenesis ability as illustrated in Figure 15 [127]. 5. Challenges and Future Perspective

Despite all the achievements made in the past in 3D printing of tissue engineering, several challenges still exist. Challenges can be divided into two major categories: (1) 3D printing of biomaterials including live cells and (2) Post-implantation integration and functionality in vivo model. One of the most common problems during manufacturing is nozzle clogging in nozzle-based 3D printing techniques. To overcome nozzle clogging, printing precursor should have proper viscosity and need to be homogenous. Another problem is that the 3D printed constructs need to be adequately stable and mechanically stiff to ensure effective prosthesis. For instance, in hard tissue transplant, the scaffolds elastic modulus should be high enough to sustain its designed porosity and structure to help natural cell growth [128].

3D printed constructs for bone tissue engineering, being eventually implanted in a body, so these constructs also need to support vascularization to deliver sufficient amount of oxygen and nutrition to the cells in vivo to enhance the growth of newly implanted bone [129]. Vascularization plays a pivotal role in a successful bone tissue implant. However, it remains a daunting challenge in bone tissue engineering, particularly, in clinical application of large bone defects. Development of vascularized and clinically applicable bone substitutes with adequate blood supply, capable of inducing angiogenesis and sustaining implant viability remains a critical challenge. Since oxygen is only accessible to those cells through diffusion that are 100‚Äì200 Œºm from blood vessels, bioprinted constructs thicker than 400 Œºm face oxygenation problem. Therefore, it is a critical task to provide ideal conditions to help vascularization in implanted bone constructs. There is a need for some extensive research to completely understand the mechanism of the biological system of bone. Thus, manufacturing a biomimetic vascularized bone that mimics the native bone can be helpful to Figure 16. Gray/brown color shows CaP implants, blue is mineralized implants and osteoid formation can be seen by orange color. Histomorphometry has done on trichrome sections (P <

0.1, where n =

5. Challenges and Future Perspective

Despite all the achievements made in the past in 3D printing of tissue engineering, several challenges still exist. Challenges can be divided into two major categories: (1) 3D printing of biomaterials including live cells and (2) Post-implantation integration and functionality in vivo model. One of the most common problems during manufacturing is nozzle clogging in nozzle-based 3D printing techniques. To overcome nozzle clogging, printing precursor should have proper viscosity and need to be homogenous. Another problem is that the 3D printed constructs need to be adequately stable and mechanically stiff to ensure eective prosthesis. For instance, in hard tissue transplant, the scaolds elastic modulus should be high enough to sustain its designed porosity and structure to help natural cell growth [128]. 3D printed constructs for bone tissue engineering, being eventually implanted in a body, so these constructs also need to support vascularization to deliver sucient amount of oxygen and nutrition to the cells in vivo to enhance the growth of newly implanted bone [129]. Vascularization plays a pivotal role in a successful bone tissue implant. However, it remains a daunting challenge in bone tissue engineering, particularly, in clinical application of large bone defects. Development of vascularized and

Materials 2019, 12, 3361 19 of 26 clinically applicable bone substitutes with adequate blood supply, capable of inducing angiogenesis and sustaining implant viability remains a critical challenge. Since oxygen is only accessible to those cells through diusion that are 100‚Äì200 m from blood vessels, bioprinted constructs thicker than 400 m face oxygenation problem. Therefore, it is a critical task to provide ideal conditions to help vascularization in implanted bone constructs. There is a need for some extensive research to completely understand the mechanism of the biological system of bone. Thus, manufacturing a biomimetic vascularized bone that mimics the native bone can be helpful to overcome these hurdles. Due to the ability of bioprinters utilizing several print-heads loaded with dierent cell types, introducing vasculature was made possible to a 3D printed construct [130‚Äì132].

Recently, nozzle-based 3D-printers enabled the printing of endothelial cells using multiple bioinks for the development of thick vascularized [133,134]. Especially, digital light processing (DLP) based

3D bioprinting can oer extraordinary speed, scalability and resolution for printing complex 3D structures with micrometer resolution [135,136]. For instance, Zhu et al. printed well-designed vascular channels without using perfusion or sacrificial materials by utilizing a rapid microscale continuous optical bioprinter (COB). In this method, glycidyl methacrylate-hyaluronic acid (GM-HA) and GelMA-cell laden bioinks were used to create channels and channel adjacent regions. From the outcomes of this study, researchers were able to demonstrate the progressive formation of endothelial network and formation of the lumen-like structures in vivo/vitro model. Anastomosis between the bioprinted endothelial network and circulation was observed with functional blood vessels featuring red blood cells [137]. Moreover, hypoxia is also having an important role in vascularization and bone regeneration process. Hypoxia belongs to the family of Hypoxia-Inducible Transcription Factors (HIFs) [138]. Kuss et al. utilized short-term hypoxic conditions to endorse vascularization in a hybrid

3D printed scaold of polycaprolactone/hydroxyapatite (PCL/HAp) and stromal vascular fraction (SVF) derived cell laden bioink [139].

Another type of challenge is regulatory hurdles, customized 3D printing technology entails series of diculties in the regulatory approval field. Though, it is urgent for the managing authorities to establish appropriate laws and regulations to ensure sustainable progress of 3D printing technology.

At present, 3D-printed sca

olds and tissues are used for evaluation and screening purposes in animal models.

6. Conclusions

In summary, this review outlined the latest researches on development of 3D printing of bioceramics for bone tissue engineering, current state of the art is also discussed. Extensive amount of research on 3D bioprinting over the past 10 years highlighted its wide range of applications and potentials in bone tissue engineering. Although, plethora of goals have been accomplished in 3D printing of bioceramics, but it is still in its emerging stage.

However, to deal with challenges such as vascularization, and printing related problems, further research on development of bioinks, integration of dierent 3D bioprinting technologies, improvement of the mechanical properties of existing bioceramics, development of composites with excellent biocompatibility and better understanding of bonding mechanism between bone mineral and collagen are some primary areas of concern that can help to improve the applications of 3D printing in bone tissue engineering. Recently, a very limited number of bio-printed products have been commercially available. Due to the rapid expansion of this industry in the last few years, it is foreseeable that more bio-printed constructs will ultimately become commercially available to help wide range of patients suering from dierent kind of diseases. The technical problems related to clinical requirements and materials selection are mentioned above, multidisciplinary research will be required to tackle those problems and to comprehensively understand the potential of bioprinting in bone tissue engineering.

„Éê„Ç§„É≥„ÉÄ„Éº„Ç∏„Çß„ÉÉ„ÉÜ„Ç£„É≥„Ç∞ÊäÄË°ì„ÅØÔºå1990Âπ¥‰ª£ÂàùÈ†≠„Å´„Éû„Çµ„ÉÅ„É•„Éº„Çª„ÉÉ„ÉÑÂ∑•ÁßëÂ§ßÂ≠¶ÔºàMITÔºâ„ÅßÈñãÁô∫„Åï„Çå„Åü„ÇÇ„ÅÆ„Åß„ÅÇ„Çã[18]„ÄÇÂõ≥2„ÅØÔºå„Éê„Ç§„É≥„ÉÄ„Éº„Ç∏„Çß„ÉÉ„ÉÜ„Ç£„É≥„Ç∞„ÅÆÊ¶ÇÁï•„ÇíÁ§∫„Åó„Åü„ÇÇ„ÅÆ„Åß„ÅÇ„Çã„ÄÇ„Åì„ÅÆÊäÄË°ì„Åß„ÅØÔºåÁ≤âÊú´Â∫ä„Åã„Çâ„Éê„Ç§„É≥„ÉÄ„Éº„ÇíÈÅ∏ÊäûÁöÑ„Å´‰ΩøÁî®„Åó„Å¶3D„Ç™„Éñ„Ç∏„Çß„ÇØ„Éà„Çí‰ΩúÊàê„Åô„Çã„ÄÇ„Éê„Ç§„É≥„ÉÄ„Éº„Ç∏„Çß„ÉÉ„ÉÜ„Ç£„É≥„Ç∞„ÅØ„ÄÅÁ≤â‰ΩìÊùêÊñô„ÇíÂç∞Âà∑„Åô„Çã„Åü„ÇÅ„ÅÆË≤¥Èáç„Å™ÊäÄË°ì„Åß„ÅÇ„Çã[19,20]„ÄÇÁ≤âÊú´„ÅÆÁ≤íÂ≠êÂæÑ„ÅØ„ÄÅ„Éê„Ç§„É≥„ÉÄ„Éº„Ç∏„Çß„ÉÉ„ÉÜ„Ç£„É≥„Ç∞„Å´„Åä„Åë„ÇãÁ≤âÊú´„ÅÆÊµÅÂãïÊÄß„Å´ÈáçË¶Å„Å™ÂΩ±Èüø„Çí‰∏é„Åà„Çã„ÄÇ

Âõ≥1.„Éá„Ç£

„Éê„Ç§„Ç™„Éû„ÉÜ„É™„Ç¢„É´„Éª„Ç¢„Éá„Ç£„ÉÜ„Ç£„Éñ„Éª„Éû„Éã„É•„Éï„Ç°„ÇØ„ÉÅ„É£„É™„É≥„Ç∞ÔºàAMÔºâ„ÅÆ„Åü„ÇÅ„ÅÆÊßò„ÄÖ„Å™„Çø„Ç§„Éó„ÅÆ„Ç¢„Çª„É´„É©„ÉºÊäÄË°ì„ÄÇ

2.1.

„Éê„Ç§„É≥„ÉÄ„Éº

„Ç∏„Çß„ÉÉ„ÉàÂô¥Â∞Ñ

„Éê„Ç§„É≥„ÉÄ„Éº„Ç∏„Çß„ÉÉ„ÉÜ„Ç£„É≥„Ç∞ÊäÄË°ì„ÅØÔºå1990Âπ¥‰ª£ÂàùÈ†≠„Å´„Éû„Çµ„ÉÅ„É•„Éº„Çª„ÉÉ„ÉÑÂ∑•ÁßëÂ§ßÂ≠¶ÔºàMITÔºâ„ÅßÈñãÁô∫„Åï„Çå„Åü„ÇÇ„ÅÆ„Åß„ÅÇ„Çã[18]„ÄÇÂõ≥2„ÅØÔºå„Éê„Ç§„É≥„ÉÄ„Éº„Ç∏„Çß„ÉÉ„ÉÜ„Ç£„É≥„Ç∞„ÅÆÊ¶ÇÁï•„ÇíÁ§∫„Åó„Åü„ÇÇ„ÅÆ„Åß„ÅÇ„Çã„ÄÇ„Åì„ÅÆÊäÄË°ì„Åß„ÅØÔºåÁ≤âÊú´Â∫ä„Åã„Çâ„Éê„Ç§„É≥„ÉÄ„Éº„ÇíÈÅ∏ÊäûÁöÑ„Å´‰ΩøÁî®„Åó„Å¶3D„Ç™„Éñ„Ç∏„Çß„ÇØ„Éà„Çí‰ΩúÊàê„Åô„Çã„ÄÇ„Éê„Ç§„É≥„ÉÄ„Éº„Ç∏„Çß„ÉÉ„ÉÜ„Ç£„É≥„Ç∞„ÅØ„ÄÅÁ≤â‰ΩìÊùêÊñô„ÇíÂç∞Âà∑„Åô„Çã„Åü„ÇÅ„ÅÆË≤¥Èáç„Å™ÊäÄË°ì„Åß„ÅÇ„Çã[19,20]„ÄÇÁ≤âÊú´„ÅÆÁ≤íÂ≠êÂæÑ„ÅØ„ÄÅ„Éê„Ç§„É≥„ÉÄ„Éº„Ç∏„Çß„ÉÉ„ÉÜ„Ç£„É≥„Ç∞„Å´„Åä„Åë„ÇãÁ≤âÊú´„ÅÆÊµÅÂãïÊÄß„Å´ÈáçË¶Å„Å™ÂΩ±Èüø„Çí‰∏é„Åà„Åæ„Åô„ÄÇMaterials 2019, 12, 3361 3 of 26 Materials 2019, 12, x FOR PEER REVIEW 3 of 29 Âõ≥2. „Éê„Ç§„É≥„ÉÄ„Éº„Ç∏„Çß„ÉÉ„ÉÜ„Ç£„É≥„Ç∞„ÅÆ„É°„Ç´„Éã„Ç∫„É†„ÅÆÊ®°ÂºèÂõ≥[18]„ÄÇ‰πæÂºè„Éê„Ç§„É≥„ÉÄ„Éº„Ç∏„Çß„ÉÉ„ÉÜ„Ç£„É≥„Ç∞„Åß„ÅØ„ÄÅÂÑ™„Çå„ÅüÊµÅÂãïÊÄß„Å®Â∞ë„Å™„ÅÑË°®Èù¢Á©ç„ÅÆ„Åü„ÇÅ„Å´„ÄÅÂ§ß„Åç„Å™„Çµ„Ç§„Ç∫„ÅÆÁ≤íÂ≠ê„ÅåÂ•Ω„Åæ„Çå„Çã„ÄÇÁ≤âÊú´„Çµ„Ç§„Ç∫„ÅØÊµÅÂãïÊÄß„Å´ÂΩ±Èüø„Åô„Çã„Å†„Åë„Åß„Å™„Åè„ÄÅÊúÄÁµÇË£ΩÂìÅ„ÅÆÂìÅË≥™„Å´„ÇÇÂ§ß„Åç„ÅèÂΩ±Èüø„Åô„Çã„ÄÇÂ§ö„Åè„ÅÆÁ†îÁ©∂ËÄÖ„Åå„ÄÅ„Éê„Ç§„É≥„ÉÄ„Éº„Ç∏„Çß„ÉÉ„ÉÜ„Ç£„É≥„Ç∞„Å´ÂæÆÁ≤âÊú´„Çí‰ΩøÁî®„Åó„Å¶„ÇÇË°®Èù¢„ÅÆÁ≤ó„Åï„ÅåÂ∞ë„Å™„ÅÑ„Å®Â†±Âëä„Åó„Å¶„ÅÑ„Çã„ÄÇÁ≤â‰Ωì„ÅÆÂΩ¢Áä∂„ÅÆÂΩ±Èüø„ÅØ„ÄÅÁ≤â‰Ωì„Çµ„Ç§„Ç∫„Å´ÊØî„Åπ„Å¶Â∞è„Åï„ÅÑ„ÄÇ„Åó„Åã„Åó„ÄÅÁêÉÁä∂„ÅÆÁ≤â‰Ωì„ÅØ„ÄÅÂàáÂ≠êÁä∂„ÅÆÁ≤â‰Ωì„Å´ÊØî„Åπ„Å¶ÊµÅÂãïÊÄß„ÅåËâØ„Åè„ÄÅÊë©Êì¶„ÅåÂ∞ë„Å™„ÅÑ[21-23]„ÄÇ

2.2. DEDÔºàDirect Energy DepositionÔºâ„Å´„Å§„ÅÑ„Å¶

Áõ¥Êé•„Ç®„Éç„É´„ÇÆ„ÉºÂ†ÜÁ©çÊ≥ï„Å´Âü∫„Å•„ÅèAMÊäÄË°ì„ÅØÔºåÂ∞è„Åï„Å™È†òÂüü„Å´„Ç®„Éç„É´„ÇÆ„Éº„ÇíÁî®„ÅÑ„Å¶Ôºå„ÉØ„Ç§„É§„ÇÑÁ≤âÊú´„Å™„Å©„ÅÆÊùêÊñô„ÇíÂêåÊôÇ„Å´Â†ÜÁ©çÔºåÊ∫∂ËûçÔºåÂõ∫Âåñ„Åï„Åõ„Çã„ÇÇ„ÅÆ„Åß„ÅÇ„Çã[24]„ÄÇÁõ¥Êé•ÁöÑ„Å™„Ç®„Éç„É´„ÇÆ„ÉºÊ∫ê„Å®„Åó„Å¶„ÅØ„ÄÅÈõªÊ∞ó„ÄÅ„Åæ„Åü„ÅØ„É¨„Éº„Ç∂„Éº„Éì„Éº„É†„Çí‰ΩøÁî®„Åó„Å¶„ÄÅÈáëÂ±û„ÄÅ„Çª„É©„Éü„ÉÉ„ÇØ„ÄÅ„Åæ„Åü„ÅØË§áÂêàÊùêÊñô„ÇíÊ∫∂Ëûç„Åô„Çã„Åì„Å®„Åå„Åß„Åç„Çã„ÄÇ„É¨„Éº„Ç∂„Éº„Éª„ÇØ„É©„ÉÉ„Éá„Ç£„É≥„Ç∞„ÄÅ„É¨„Éº„Ç∂„Éº„Éª„Ç®„É≥„Ç∏„Éã„Ç¢„Éâ„Éª„Éç„ÉÉ„Éà„Éª„Ç∑„Çß„Ç§„Éî„É≥„Ç∞ÔºàLENS‚Ñ¢Ôºâ„ÄÅ„É¨„Éº„Ç∂„Éº„Éª„É°„É´„Éà„Éª„Ç§„É≥„Ç∏„Çß„ÇØ„Ç∑„Éß„É≥„Å™„Å©„ÅÆ„É¨„Éº„Ç∂„Éº„Éª„Ç¢„Ç∑„Çπ„Éà„Éª„ÉÄ„Ç§„É¨„ÇØ„Éà„Éª„Éá„Éù„Ç∏„Ç∑„Éß„É≥ÊäÄË°ì„ÅØ„ÄÅ„Åì„ÅÆÊäÄË°ì„ÅÆ‰∏ÄËà¨ÁöÑ„Å™‰æã„Åß„Åô„ÄÇ„Çª„É©„Éü„ÉÉ„ÇØÁõ¥Êé•„Ç®„Éç„É´„ÇÆ„ÉºÂ†ÜÁ©çÊ≥ïÔºàDEDÔºâ„Åß„ÅØ„ÄÅË£ÖÁΩÆ„ÅÆÂç∞Âà∑„Éò„ÉÉ„Éâ„Å´„Éé„Ç∫„É´„Åå„ÅÇ„Çä„ÄÅ„Çª„É©„Éü„ÉÉ„ÇØÁ≤âÊú´Á≤íÂ≠ê„Çí„É¨„Éº„Ç∂„Éº„Éì„Éº„É†„ÅÆÁÑ¶ÁÇπ„Å´‰æõÁµ¶„Åó„Åæ„Åô„ÄÇ„Çª„É©„Éü„ÉÉ„ÇØÁ≤âÊú´„ÅØÔºåÂü∫Êùø‰∏ä„ÅßÂ±§Áä∂„Å´Ê∫∂Ëûç„ÉªÂõ∫Âåñ„Åô„Çã[25]„ÄÇÂõ≥3„ÅØÔºåLENS„ÅÆÊ®°ÂºèÂõ≥„Åß„ÅÇ„Çã[26]„ÄÇÂõ≥2. „Éê„Ç§„É≥„ÉÄ„ÉºÂô¥Âá∫Ê©üÊßã„ÅÆÊ®°ÂºèÂõ≥[18]„ÄÇ

„Éâ„É©„Ç§„Éê„Ç§„É≥„ÉÄ„Éº„ÅÆÂô¥Â∞Ñ„Å´„ÅØ„ÄÅÂÑ™„Çå„ÅüÊµÅÂãïÊÄß„Å®Â∞ë„Å™„ÅÑË°®Èù¢Á©ç„ÅÆ„Åü„ÇÅ„Å´„ÄÅÂ§ß„Åç„Å™„Çµ„Ç§„Ç∫„ÅÆÁ≤íÂ≠ê„ÅåÂ•Ω„Åæ„Çå„Åæ„Åô„ÄÇÁ≤íÂ≠êÂæÑ„ÅØÊµÅÂãïÊÄß„Å´ÂΩ±Èüø„Åô„Çã„Å†„Åë„Åß„Å™„Åè„ÄÅÊúÄÁµÇË£ΩÂìÅ„ÅÆÂìÅË≥™„Å´„ÇÇÂ§ß„Åç„ÅèÂΩ±Èüø„Åó„Åæ„Åô„ÄÇÂ§ö„Åè„ÅÆÁ†îÁ©∂ËÄÖ„Åå„ÄÅ„Éê„Ç§„É≥„ÉÄ„Éº„Ç∏„Çß„ÉÉ„ÉÜ„Ç£„É≥„Ç∞„Å´ÂæÆÁ≤âÊú´„Çí‰ΩøÁî®„Åó„Å¶„ÇÇË°®Èù¢„ÅÆÁ≤ó„Åï„ÅåÂ∞ë„Å™„ÅÑ„Å®Â†±Âëä„Åó„Å¶„ÅÑ„Çã„ÄÇÁ≤â‰Ωì„ÅÆÂΩ¢Áä∂„ÅÆÂΩ±Èüø„ÅØ„ÄÅÁ≤â‰Ωì„ÅÆ„Çµ„Ç§„Ç∫„Å´ÊØî„Åπ„Å¶Â∞è„Åï„ÅÑ„ÄÇ„Åó„Åã„Åó„ÄÅÁêÉÁä∂„ÅÆÁ≤â‰Ωì„ÅØ„ÄÅÂàáÂ≠êÁä∂„ÅÆÁ≤â‰Ωì„Å´ÊØî„Åπ„Å¶„ÄÅ„Çà„ÇäËâØ„ÅÑÊµÅÂãïÊÄß„Å®„Çà„ÇäÂ∞ë„Å™„ÅÑÊë©Êì¶„ÇíÊúâ„Åó„Å¶„ÅÑ„Çã[21-23]„ÄÇ

2.2.

Direct Energy DepositionÔºàDEDÔºâ„Å´„Å§„ÅÑ„Å¶

Áõ¥Êé•„Ç®„Éç„É´„ÇÆ„ÉºÂ†ÜÁ©çÊ≥ï„Å´Âü∫„Å•„ÅèAMÊäÄË°ì„ÅØÔºåÂ∞è„Åï„Å™È†òÂüü„Å´„Ç®„Éç„É´„ÇÆ„Éº„ÇíÁî®„ÅÑ„Å¶Ôºå„ÉØ„Ç§„É§„ÇÑÁ≤âÊú´„Å™„Å©„ÅÆÊùêÊñô„ÇíÂêåÊôÇ„Å´Â†ÜÁ©çÔºåÊ∫∂ËûçÔºåÂõ∫Âåñ„Åï„Åõ„Çã„ÇÇ„ÅÆ„Åß„ÅÇ„Çã[24]„ÄÇÁõ¥Êé•ÁöÑ„Å™„Ç®„Éç„É´„ÇÆ„ÉºÊ∫ê„Å®„Åó„Å¶„ÅØ„ÄÅÈõªÊ∞ó„ÄÅ„Åæ„Åü„ÅØ„É¨„Éº„Ç∂„Éº„Éì„Éº„É†„Çí‰ΩøÁî®„Åó„Å¶„ÄÅÈáëÂ±û„ÄÅ„Çª„É©„Éü„ÉÉ„ÇØ„ÄÅ„Åæ„Åü„ÅØË§áÂêàÊùêÊñô„ÇíÊ∫∂Ëûç„Åô„Çã„Åì„Å®„Åå„Åß„Åç„Çã„ÄÇ„É¨„Éº„Ç∂„Éº„ÇØ„É©„ÉÉ„Éá„Ç£„É≥„Ç∞„ÄÅ„É¨„Éº„Ç∂„Éº„Ç®„É≥„Ç∏„Éã„Ç¢„Éº„Éâ„Éç„ÉÉ„Éà„Ç∑„Çß„Ç§„Éî„É≥„Ç∞ÔºàLENSÔºâ„ÄÅ„É¨„Éº„Ç∂„Éº„É°„É´„Éà„Ç§„É≥„Ç∏„Çß„ÇØ„Ç∑„Éß„É≥„Å™„Å©„ÅÆ„É¨„Éº„Ç∂„Éº„Ç¢„Ç∑„Çπ„ÉàÁõ¥Êé•ÊàêËÜúÊäÄË°ì„ÅØ„ÄÅ„Åì„ÅÆÊäÄË°ì„ÅÆ‰∏ÄËà¨ÁöÑ„Å™‰æã„Åß„Åô„ÄÇ

„Çª„É©„Éü„ÉÉ„ÇØÁõ¥Êé•„Ç®„Éç„É´„ÇÆ„ÉºÂ†ÜÁ©çÊ≥ïÔºàDEDÔºâ„Åß„ÅØÔºåË£ÖÁΩÆ„ÅÆÂç∞Âà∑„Éò„ÉÉ„Éâ„Å´„Éé„Ç∫„É´„Åå„ÅÇ„ÇäÔºå„Çª„É©„Éü„ÉÉ„ÇØÁ≤âÊú´Á≤íÂ≠ê„Çí„É¨„Éº„Ç∂„Éº„Éì„Éº„É†„ÅÆÁÑ¶ÁÇπ„Å´‰æõÁµ¶„Åô„Çã„ÄÇ„Çª„É©„Éü„ÉÉ„ÇØÁ≤âÊú´„ÅØÔºåÂü∫Êùø‰∏ä„ÅßÂ±§Áä∂„Å´Ê∫∂Ëûç„ÉªÂõ∫Âåñ„Åô„Çã[25]„ÄÇÂõ≥3„ÅØÔºåLENS„ÅÆÊ®°ÂºèÂõ≥„Åß„ÅÇ„Çã[26]„ÄÇ

‰ªñ„Å´„ÇÇ„ÄÅ‰∏ÄÈÉ®„ÅÆSLA„Éó„É≠„Çª„Çπ„ÅÆ„É©„Ç§„Éà„Éî„ÇØ„Çª„É´„ÅåÈù¢ÂÜÖÂæÆÁ¥∞ÊßãÈÄ†„ÅÆÊßãÁØâ„ÇíÂà∂Èôê„Åô„Çã„Å™„Å©„ÅÆÂïèÈ°å„Åå„ÅÇ„Çä„Åæ„Åô„ÄÇÈñìÊé•SLAÊ≥ï„ÅØ„Åì„ÅÆÂïèÈ°å„ÇíÂÖãÊúç„Åó„Å¶„ÅÑ„Çã„Åå„ÄÅ„Ç≥„Çπ„Éà„ÄÅÊôÇÈñì„ÄÅÊùêÊñô„ÇíË¶Å„Åô„Çã„Éó„É≠„Çª„Çπ„Åß„ÅÇ„Çã[43]„ÄÇLi„Çâ„ÅØÔºåÈñìÊé•ÁöÑ„Å™„Çπ„ÉÜ„É¨„Ç™„É™„ÇΩ„Ç∞„É©„Éï„Ç£„ÇíÁî®„ÅÑ„Å¶„Éû„Ç§„ÇØ„É≠„Éù„Éº„É©„Çπ-TCP„ÇíË£ΩÈÄ†„Åó„Å¶„ÅÑ„ÇãÔºé„Åì„ÅÆÊâãÊ≥ï„ÅßÊ®πËÑÇÂûã„Çí‰ΩúÊàê„ÅóÔºå„Çª„É©„Éü„ÉÉ„ÇØ„Ç≤„É´Èã≥ÈÄ†Áî®„ÅÆÊ∞¥ÊÄßÁÜ±Á°¨ÂåñÊÄß„Çª„É©„Éü„ÉÉ„ÇØÊá∏ÊøÅÊ∂≤„ÇíÂÖÖÂ°´„Åó„Åü„ÄÇÈã≥Âûã„ÅÆÈô§Âéª„Å´„ÅØÁÜ±Âá¶ÁêÜÂ∑•Á®ã„ÇíÁî®„ÅÑ„Åü„ÄÇ„Åù„ÅÆÁµêÊûúÔºåÁÑºÁµêÂæå„ÅÆTCP„Çπ„Ç´„Ç¶„É´„ÅØÔºåÊâÄÊúõ„ÅÆÂ§öÂ≠îÊÄßÔºåÂΩ¢Áä∂„ÇíÁ§∫„ÅóÔºå„Çà„ÇäÈ´ò„ÅÑÂº∑Â∫¶„ÅåÂæó„Çâ„Çå„Åü„Å®ÁµêË´ñ„Å•„Åë„Çâ„Çå„Åü[44]„ÄÇ

3.1. „Éè„Ç§„Éâ„É≠„Ç≠„Ç∑„Ç¢„Éë„Çø„Ç§„Éà(HA)

„Éè„Ç§„Éâ„É≠„Ç≠„Ç∑„Ç¢„Éë„Çø„Ç§„Éà(HA)„ÅØ„ÄÅCa(PO)(OH)„Å®„Åó„Å¶ÊèèÂÜô„Åï„Çå„ÄÅÈ™®ÈáèÂÖ®‰Ωì„ÅÆ„Åª„Åº65%„ÇíÂç†„ÇÅ„Çã„Å®„Åï„Çå„Çã„ÄÇ„Éè„Ç§„Éâ„É≠„Ç≠„Ç∑„Ç¢„Éë„Çø„Ç§„Éà„ÅØ„ÄÅ‰ªñ„ÅÆ„É™„É≥ÈÖ∏„Ç´„É´„Ç∑„Ç¶„É†„Å®ÊØîËºÉ„Åó„Å¶„ÄÅCa/PÊØî„Åå1.67„Å®Â•Ω„Åæ„Åó„ÅÑ„Åì„Å®„Åã„Çâ„ÄÅÊØíÊÄß„Åå‰Ωé„Åè„ÄÅÂÆâÂÆö„Åó„Å¶„ÅÑ„Çã„ÄÇ„Éè„Ç§„Éâ„É≠„Ç≠„Ç∑„Ç¢„Éë„Çø„Ç§„Éà„ÅØ„ÄÅ‰∫∫Èñì„ÅÆÈ™®„ÇÑÊ≠Ø„ÅÆ‰∏ªË¶Å„Å™ÁÑ°Ê©üË≥™ÈÉ®ÂàÜ„Åß„ÅÇ„Çä„ÄÅÁ°¨ÁµÑÁπîÁΩÆÊèõÁî®„ÅÆ„Éê„Ç§„Ç™„Çª„É©„Éü„ÉÉ„ÇØ„Çπ„ÅÆÁâπÊÄß„ÇÑÊñ∞„Åó„ÅÑÂøúÁî®„ÇíÈñãÁô∫„Åô„Çã„Åü„ÇÅ„Å´Áî®„ÅÑ„Çâ„Çå„Å¶„ÅÑ„Çã[65-68]„ÄÇÂ§ö„Åè„ÅÆÁ†îÁ©∂ËÄÖ„Åå„ÄÅÈ™®„ÇÑÊ≠Ø„ÅÆÁßªÊ§ç„Å´HA„Çπ„Ç´„Éï„Ç©„Éº„É´„Éâ„Çí‰ΩøÁî®„Åó„Åü„Åì„Å®„ÇíÂ†±Âëä„Åó„Å¶„ÅÑ„Çã[69-77]„ÄÇ„É¨„Éº„Ç∂„Éº„Çπ„ÉÜ„É¨„Ç™„É™„ÇΩ„Ç∞„É©„Éï„Ç£„Éº„ÅØ„ÄÅË§áÈõë„Å™HA„Éë„Éº„ÉÑ„ÇíË£ΩÈÄ†„Åô„Çã„Åü„ÇÅ„ÅÆÊúÄ„ÇÇÂäπÊûúÁöÑ„ÅßÈ†ªÁπÅ„Å´‰ΩøÁî®„Åï„Çå„ÇãAMÊäÄË°ì„ÅÆ‰∏Ä„Å§„Å®„Åó„Å¶Ë™çË≠ò„Åï„Çå„Å¶„ÅÑ„Çã„ÄÇBarry„Çâ„ÅØ„ÄÅ„Éò„É™„Ç¶„É†-„Ç´„Éâ„Éü„Ç¶„É†ÔºàHeCdÔºâ„Éô„Éº„Çπ„ÅÆ„É¨„Éº„Ç∂„ÉºÊäÄË°ì„ÇíÁî®„ÅÑ„Å¶„ÄÅHA„Éô„Éº„Çπ„ÅÆ„Ç™„É™„Ç¥„Ç´„Éº„Éú„Éç„Éº„Éà„Ç∏„É°„Çø„ÇØ„É™„É¨„Éº„ÉàÔºàOCM-2Ôºâ„ÅÆË§áÂêà„Çπ„Ç´„Éï„Ç©„Éº„É´„Éâ„Çí‰ΩúË£Ω„Åó„Åü„ÄÇ„É¨„Éº„Ç∂„ÉºÂä†Â∑•„Åï„Çå„ÅüHA„Çπ„Ç´„Éï„Ç©„Éº„É´„Éâ„ÅØ„ÄÅ„Çπ„Ç´„Éï„Ç©„Éº„É´„ÉâÂÜÖ„Åß„ÅÆÁ¥∞ËÉû„ÅÆ‰ªòÁùÄ„ÅåÂº∑Âåñ„Åï„Çå„Åü„Å®„ÅÑ„ÅÜ„ÄÇ„É¨„Éº„Ç∂„ÉºÂä†Â∑•„Å´„Çà„Çä„ÄÅË∂ÖËá®Áïå‰∫åÈÖ∏ÂåñÁÇ≠Á¥†ÔºàscCO2Ôºâ„ÇíÁî®„ÅÑ„Å¶ÊúâÂÆ≥„Å™ÊÆãÁïôÁâ©„ÇíÂäπÊûúÁöÑ„Å´Èô§Âéª„Åó„ÄÅ„Çπ„Ç´„Éï„Ç©„Éº„É´„Éâ„ÇíÁîü‰ΩìÈÅ©ÂêàÊÄß„ÅÆ„ÅÇ„Çã„ÇÇ„ÅÆ„Å´„Åó„Åü„ÄÇscCO2„ÅßÂá¶ÁêÜ„Åï„Çå„ÅüHA„Éô„Éº„Çπ„ÅÆË§áÂêàÊùêÊñô„ÅØ„ÄÅvivo„Å®vitro„ÅÆ‰∏°Êñπ„ÅÆÁ†îÁ©∂„ÅßÁ¥∞ËÉû„ÅÆ„Çà„ÇäËâØ„ÅÑ‰ªòÁùÄ„ÇíÁ§∫„Åó„Åü[78]„ÄÇ„Åî„ÅèÊúÄËøë„ÅÆÁ†îÁ©∂„Åß„ÅØ„ÄÅ„Éä„Éé„Éè„Ç§„Éâ„É≠„Ç≠„Ç∑„Ç¢„Éë„Çø„Ç§„ÉàÔºànHAÔºâ„Å®ËÑ±„Çø„É≥„Éë„ÇØÁâõÈ™®ÔºàDBBÔºâ„Å®„ÅÑ„ÅÜ2Á®ÆÈ°û„ÅÆ„Éè„Ç§„Éâ„É≠„Ç≠„Ç∑„Ç¢„Éë„Çø„Ç§„Éà„Çí„Ç≥„É©„Éº„Ç≤„É≥„Å´ÂàÜÊï£„Åï„Åõ„ÄÅ3D„Éó„É™„É≥„ÉàÁî®„ÅÆ„Éê„Ç§„Ç™„Ç§„É≥„ÇØ„ÇíË™øË£Ω„Åó„Åü„ÄÇ„Åì„Çå„Å´„Çà„Çä„ÄÅ3D„Éó„É™„É≥„Éà„ÅßÂ§öÂ≠îË≥™ÊßãÈÄ†„ÇíÂΩ¢Êàê„Åó„Åü„ÄÇ„Åì„Çå„Çâ„ÅÆÊùêÊñô„ÅÆÂåñÂ≠¶ÁöÑ„ÉªÁâ©ÁêÜÁöÑÁâπÊÄß„ÄÅÁîü‰ΩìÈÅ©ÂêàÊÄß„ÄÅ„Éí„ÉàÈ™®È´ÑÁî±Êù•ÈñìÂÖÖÁπîÂππÁ¥∞ËÉûÔºàhBMSCsÔºâ„ÅÆÈ™®ÂΩ¢ÊàêÂàÜÂåñ„Å∏„ÅÆÂΩ±Èüø„Å™„Å©„ÇíË™ø„Åπ„Åü„ÄÇnHA/CoL„Åä„Çà„Å≥DBB/CoL„Éê„Ç§„Ç™„Ç§„É≥„ÇØ„ÅÆ‰∏°Êñπ„Çí‰ΩøÁî®„Åó„Å¶Ôºå„Éê„Ç§„Ç™„Éü„Éü„ÉÉ„ÇØ„Å™3DË∂≥Â†¥„ÇíÂäπÊûúÁöÑ„Å´Âç∞Âà∑„Åó„Åü„ÄÇ„Åì„ÅÆÁ†îÁ©∂„ÅÆÁµêÊûú„ÄÅhBMSC„ÅÆÂ¢óÊÆñ„Å®ÂàÜÂåñ„ÇíÂä©„Åë„Çã2Á®ÆÈ°û„ÅÆ„Éè„Ç§„Éâ„É≠„Ç≠„Ç∑„Ç¢„Éë„Çø„Ç§„ÉàË§áÂêà‰Ωì„Åå„ÄÅ3D„Çπ„Ç´„Éï„Ç©„Éº„É´„Éâ„ÅÆ„Éê„Ç§„Ç™„Ç§„É≥„ÇØ„Å®„Åó„Å¶ÊúâÊúõ„Å™ÂÄôË£ú„Åß„ÅÇ„Çã„Åì„Å®„ÅåË®ºÊòé„Åï„Çå„Åü[79]„ÄÇWoeszn„Çâ„ÅØ„ÄÅ„Çπ„ÉÜ„É¨„Ç™„É™„ÇΩ„Ç∞„É©„Éï„Ç£„Éº„Å®„Çª„É©„Éü„ÉÉ„ÇØ„Ç≤„É´Âåñ„ÇíÁµÑ„ÅøÂêà„Çè„Åõ„Å¶„ÄÅÂ≠îÂæÑ450Œºm„ÅÆÂæÆÂ§öÂ≠îÊÄßHA„Çπ„Ç´„Éï„Ç©„Éº„É´„Éâ„Çí‰ΩúË£Ω„Åó„Åü„ÄÇÊ∞¥ÊÄß„ÅÆÁÜ±Á°¨ÂåñÊÄß„Çπ„É©„É™„Éº„ÇíÂÖÖÂ°´„Åó„ÅüÊÑüÂÖâÊÄß„ÅÆÊ∂≤Áä∂Ê®πËÑÇ„ÇíÂûã„Å´Áî®„ÅÑ„Åü„ÄÇÂûã„ÅÆÊ®πËÑÇ„Å®ÁÑºÁµê„ÇíÁÑº„ÅÑ„Å¶Âõ≥8„ÇíÂÆüÁèæ„Åó„Åü„ÄÇÈ™®ÁµÑÁπîÂ∑•Â≠¶„ÅÆÊ®°ÂºèÂõ≥[64]„ÄÇ

3.1.

„Éè„Ç§„Éâ„É≠„Ç≠„Ç∑„Ç¢„Éë„Çø„Ç§„ÉàÔºàHA

„Éè„Ç§„Éâ„É≠„Ç≠„Ç∑„Ç¢„Éë„Çø„Ç§„Éà(HA)„ÅØ„ÄÅCa10(PO4)6(OH)2„Å®„Åó„Å¶Ë°®„Åï„Çå„ÄÅÈ™®ÈáèÂÖ®‰Ωì„ÅÆÁ¥Ñ65%„ÇíÂç†„ÇÅ„Çã„Å®„Åï„Çå„Çã„ÄÇ„Éè„Ç§„Éâ„É≠„Ç≠„Ç∑„Ç¢„Éë„Çø„Ç§„Éà„ÅØ„ÄÅ‰ªñ„ÅÆ„É™„É≥ÈÖ∏„Ç´„É´„Ç∑„Ç¶„É†„Å®ÊØîËºÉ„Åó„Å¶„ÄÅCa/PÊØî„Åå1.67„Å®Â•Ω„Åæ„Åó„ÅÑ„Åì„Å®„Åã„Çâ„ÄÅÊØíÊÄß„Åå‰Ωé„Åè„ÄÅÂÆâÂÆö„Åó„Å¶„ÅÑ„Çã„ÄÇ„Éè„Ç§„Éâ„É≠„Ç≠„Ç∑„Ç¢„Éë„Çø„Ç§„Éà„ÅØ„ÄÅ‰∫∫Èñì„ÅÆÈ™®„ÇÑÊ≠Ø„ÅÆ‰∏ªË¶Å„Å™ÁÑ°Ê©üË≥™ÈÉ®ÂàÜ„Åß„ÅÇ„Çä„ÄÅÁ°¨ÁµÑÁπîÁΩÆÊèõ„ÅÆ„Åü„ÇÅ„ÅÆ„Éê„Ç§„Ç™„Çª„É©„Éü„ÉÉ„ÇØ„Çπ„ÅÆÁâπÊÄß„ÇÑÊñ∞„Åó„ÅÑÁî®ÈÄî„ÇíÈñãÁô∫„Åô„Çã„Åü„ÇÅ„Å´Áî®„ÅÑ„Çâ„Çå„Å¶„ÅÑ„Çã[65-68]„ÄÇÂ§ö„Åè„ÅÆÁ†îÁ©∂ËÄÖ„Åå„ÄÅÈ™®„ÇÑÊ≠Ø„ÅÆÁßªÊ§ç„Å´HA„ÅÆ„Çπ„Ç´„Ç™„Éº„É´„Éâ„Çí‰ΩøÁî®„Åó„Åü„Åì„Å®„ÇíÂ†±Âëä„Åó„Å¶„ÅÑ„Çã[69-77]„ÄÇ

„É¨„Éº„Ç∂„Éº„Çπ„ÉÜ„É¨„Ç™„É™„ÇΩ„Ç∞„É©„Éï„Ç£„ÅØ„ÄÅÊúÄ„ÇÇÂäπÊûúÁöÑ„ÅßÈ†ªÁπÅ„Å´‰ΩøÁî®„Åï„Çå„ÇãÊäÄË°ì„ÅÆ‰∏Ä„Å§„Åß„ÅÇ„Çã„Å®Ë™çË≠ò„Åï„Çå„Å¶„ÅÑ„Åæ„Åô„ÄÇ

Barry„Çâ„ÅØ„ÄÅ„Éò„É™„Ç¶„É†-„Ç´„Éâ„Éü„Ç¶„É†ÔºàHeCdÔºâ„Éô„Éº„Çπ„ÅÆ„É¨„Éº„Ç∂„ÉºÊäÄË°ì„ÇíÁî®„ÅÑ„Å¶„ÄÅHA„Éô„Éº„Çπ„ÅÆ„Ç™„É™„Ç¥„Ç´„Éº„Éú„Éç„Éº„Éà„Ç∏„É°„Çø„ÇØ„É™„É¨„Éº„ÉàÔºàOCM-2ÔºâË§áÂêà„Çπ„Ç´„Ç™„Éº„É´„Éâ„Çí‰ΩúË£Ω„Åó„Åü„ÄÇ„Åù„ÅÆÁµêÊûú„ÄÅ„É¨„Éº„Ç∂„Éº„ÇíÁî®„ÅÑ„ÅüHA„Çπ„Ç≥„Éº„É´„Éâ„ÅØ„ÄÅ„Çπ„Ç≥„Éº„É´„ÉâÂÜÖ„Åß„ÅÆÁ¥∞ËÉû„ÅÆ‰ªòÁùÄ„ÇíÂº∑Âåñ„Åô„Çã„Åì„Å®„Åå„Åß„Åç„Åü„ÄÇ„É¨„Éº„Ç∂„ÉºÂä†Â∑•„Å´„Çà„Çä„ÄÅË∂ÖËá®Áïå‰∫åÈÖ∏ÂåñÁÇ≠Á¥†ÔºàscCO2Ôºâ„ÇíÁî®„ÅÑ„Å¶ÊúâÂÆ≥„Å™ÊÆãÁïôÁâ©„ÅåÂäπÊûúÁöÑ„Å´Èô§Âéª„Åï„Çå„ÄÅ„Çπ„Ç≥„Éº„É´„Éâ„ÅÆÁîü‰ΩìÈÅ©ÂêàÊÄß„ÅåÂêë‰∏ä„Åó„Åü„ÄÇscCO2„ÅßÂá¶ÁêÜ„Åï„Çå„ÅüHA„Éô„Éº„Çπ„ÅÆË§áÂêàÊùêÊñô„ÅØ„ÄÅvivo„Å®vitro„ÅÆ‰∏°Êñπ„ÅÆÁ†îÁ©∂„ÅßÁ¥∞ËÉû„ÅÆ„Çà„ÇäËâØ„ÅÑ‰ªòÁùÄ„ÇíÁ§∫„Åó„Åü[78]„ÄÇ„Åî„ÅèÊúÄËøë„ÅÆÁ†îÁ©∂„Åß„ÅØ„ÄÅ„Éä„Éé„Éè„Ç§„Éâ„É≠„Ç≠„Ç∑„Ç¢„Éë„Çø„Ç§„ÉàÔºànHAÔºâ„Å®ËÑ±„Çø„É≥„Éë„ÇØÁâõÈ™®ÔºàDBBÔºâ„Å®„ÅÑ„ÅÜ2„Å§„ÅÆÁï∞„Å™„Çã„Çø„Ç§„Éó„ÅÆ„Éè„Ç§„Éâ„É≠„Ç≠„Ç∑„Ç¢„Éë„Çø„Ç§„Éà„Çí„Ç≥„É©„Éº„Ç≤„É≥„Å´ÂàÜÊï£„Åï„Åõ„Å¶„ÄÅ3D„Éó„É™„É≥„ÉàÁî®„ÅÆ„Éê„Ç§„Ç™„Ç§„É≥„ÇØ„ÇíË™øË£Ω„Åó„Åü„ÄÇ„Åì„Çå„Å´„Çà„Çä„ÄÅ3D„Éó„É™„É≥„Éà„ÅßÂ§öÂ≠îË≥™ÊßãÈÄ†„ÇíÂΩ¢Êàê„Åó„Åü„ÄÇ„Åì„Çå„Çâ„ÅÆÊùêÊñô„ÅÆÂåñÂ≠¶ÁöÑ„Åä„Çà„Å≥Áâ©ÁêÜÁöÑÁâπÊÄß„ÄÅÁîü‰ΩìÈÅ©ÂêàÊÄß„ÄÅ„Åä„Çà„Å≥„Éí„ÉàÈ™®È´ÑÁî±Êù•ÈñìÂÖÖÁπîÂππÁ¥∞ËÉûÔºàhBMSCsÔºâ„ÅÆÈ™®ÂΩ¢ÊàêÂàÜÂåñ„Å´Âèä„Åº„ÅôÂΩ±Èüø„ÇíË™ø„Åπ„Åü„ÄÇnHA/CoL„Åä„Çà„Å≥DBB/CoL„Éê„Ç§„Ç™„Ç§„É≥„ÇØ„Çí‰ΩøÁî®„Åó„Å¶Ôºå„Éê„Ç§„Ç™„Éü„Éü„ÉÉ„ÇØ3D„Çπ„Ç≥„Éº„É´„Éâ„ÇíÂäπÊûúÁöÑ„Å´Âç∞Âà∑„Åó„Åü„ÄÇ„Åì„ÅÆÁ†îÁ©∂„ÅÆÁµêÊûú„ÄÅhBMSC„ÅÆÂ¢óÊÆñ„Å®ÂàÜÂåñ„ÇíÂä©„Åë„Çã2Á®ÆÈ°û„ÅÆ„Éè„Ç§„Éâ„É≠„Ç≠„Ç∑„Ç¢„Éë„Çø„Ç§„ÉàË§áÂêà‰Ωì„Åå„ÄÅ3D„Çπ„Ç≥„Éº„É´„Éâ„ÅÆ„Éê„Ç§„Ç™„Ç§„É≥„ÇØ„ÅÆÊúâÊúõ„Å™ÂÄôË£ú„Åß„ÅÇ„Çã„Åì„Å®„ÅåË®ºÊòé„Åï„Çå„Åü[79]„ÄÇWoeszn„Çâ„ÅØ„ÄÅ„Çπ„ÉÜ„É¨„Ç™„É™„ÇΩ„Ç∞„É©„Éï„Ç£„Éº„Å®„Çª„É©„Éü„ÉÉ„ÇØ„Ç≤„É´Âåñ„ÇíÁµÑ„ÅøÂêà„Çè„Åõ„Å¶„ÄÅÂ≠îÂæÑ450m„ÅÆÂæÆÂ§öÂ≠îÊÄßHA„Çπ„Ç´„Ç™„Éº„É´„Éâ„Çí‰ΩúË£Ω„Åó„Åü„ÄÇÊ∞¥ÊÄß„ÅÆÁÜ±Á°¨ÂåñÊÄß„Çπ„É©„É™„Éº„ÇíÂÖÖÂ°´„Åó„ÅüÊÑüÂÖâÊÄß„ÅÆÊ∂≤Áä∂Ê®πËÑÇ„ÇíÂûã„Å´Áî®„ÅÑ„Åü„ÄÇÈã≥Âûã„ÅÆÊ®πËÑÇ„Å®ÁÑºÁµê„ÅØ„ÄÅÊâÄÊúõ„ÅÆÁâπÂæ¥„ÇíÂæó„Çã„Åü„ÇÅ„Å´ÁÑº„Åã„Çå„Åü„ÄÇÊúÄÁµÇÁöÑ„Å™Scaold„ÅØÔºåÂõ≥9„Å´Á§∫„Åô„Çà„ÅÜ„Å´ÂçìË∂ä„Åó„ÅüÈ™®ÂΩ¢Êàê„ÇíÈÅîÊàê„Åô„Çã„Åü„ÇÅ„Å´ÔºåÁ¥∞ËÉû„ÅÆÊ∑±„ÅÑÊµ∏ÈÄè„ÅÆ‰∏ã„ÅßMC3T3-E1Á¥∞ËÉû 3.2. „É™„É≥ÈÖ∏‰∏â„Ç´„É´„Ç∑„Ç¶„É†ÔºàTCP

3.2.

„É™„É≥ÈÖ∏‰∏â„Ç´„É´„Ç∑„Ç¶„É†ÔºàTCP

-TCP„Çπ„Ç´„Ç™„Éº„É´„Éâ„Çí‰ΩøÁî®„Åó„Åü„ÄÇÂÖçÁñ´Âà∂Âæ°ËÉΩÂäõ„ÅØ„Éû„ÇØ„É≠„Éï„Ç°„Éº„Ç∏„Åß„ÉÜ„Çπ„Éà„Åï„Çå„ÄÅÈ™®ÂΩ¢ÊàêËÉΩÂäõ„ÅØ„Ç≥„Éº„ÉÜ„Ç£„É≥„Ç∞„Åï„Çå„Åü„Çπ„Ç≥„Éº„É´„Éâ„ÅÆ„É©„ÉÉ„ÉàÈ™®È´Ñ„Çπ„Éà„É≠„Éº„ÉûÁ¥∞ËÉû„Åß„ÉÜ„Çπ„Éà„Åï„Çå„Åü„ÄÇ„Åì„ÅÆ„Çπ„Ç≥„Éº„É´„Éâ„ÅØ„ÄÅÂæìÊù•„ÅÆË£ΩÈÄ†ÊñπÊ≥ï„Åß‰Ωú„Çâ„Çå„Åü„Çπ„Ç≥„Éº„É´„Éâ„Å®ÊØîËºÉ„Åó„Å¶„ÄÅÈ™®ÂΩ¢Êàê„ÅåÊîπÂñÑ„Åï„Çå„ÄÅÁ¥∞ËÉûÈÅ©ÂêàÊÄß„ÅåÂêë‰∏ä„Åó„ÄÅ„Åï„Çâ„Å´ÂÖçÁñ´ÂèçÂøú„ÅÆË™øÊï¥„Å´„ÇÇÂΩπÁ´ã„Å§„Åì„Å®„Åå„Çè„Åã„Å£„Åü„ÄÇÂõ≥11„ÅØ„ÄÅ3D„Éó„É™„É≥„Éà„Åï„Çå„Åü„Çπ„Ç≥„Éº„É´„Éâ„Å®„Åù„ÅÆÁ¥∞ËÉûÁîüÂ≠òÁéá„ÇíÁ§∫„Åó„Åü„ÇÇ„ÅÆ„Åß„ÅÇ„Çã[90]„ÄÇ

Bian„Çâ„ÅØ„ÄÅÊñ∞„Åó„ÅÑÂÖâÈÄ†ÂΩ¢Ê≥ï„ÇíÁî®„ÅÑ„Å¶È™®ËªüÈ™®„ÅÆŒ≤-„É™„É≥ÈÖ∏‰∏â„Ç´„É´„Ç∑„Ç¶„É†Ôºè„Ç≥„É©„Éº„Ç≤„É≥Ë§áÂêà‰Ωì„Çí‰ΩúË£Ω„Åó„Åü„ÄÇ„Åì„ÅÆ„Éê„Ç§„Ç™„Ç§„É≥„Çπ„Éë„Ç§„Ç¢„Åï„Çå„Åü„Çπ„Ç´„Ç™„Éº„É´„Éâ„ÅØ„ÄÅ(-TCP)„Å®„Çø„Ç§„ÉóI„Ç≥„É©„Éº„Ç≤„É≥„ÇíÁî®„ÅÑ„Åü„Çª„É©„Éü„ÉÉ„ÇØ„Çπ„ÉÜ„É¨„Ç™„É™„ÇΩ„Ç∞„É©„Éï„Ç£„Éº(CSL)„Å®„Ç≤„É´„Ç≠„É£„Çπ„ÉÜ„Ç£„É≥„Ç∞„ÅÆÁµÑ„ÅøÂêà„Çè„Åõ„Å´„Çà„Å£„Å¶Ë£ΩÈÄ†„Åï„Çå„Åü„ÄÇËªüÈ™®„Å®È™®„ÅÆÈñì„ÅÆÂΩ¢ÊÖãÂ≠¶ÁöÑÁâπÊÄß„ÇíË™ø„Åπ„Çã„Åü„ÇÅ„Å´„ÄÅÁµÑÁπîÂ≠¶ÁöÑÊ§úÊüª„ÇíË°å„Å£„Åü„ÄÇ„Åì„ÅÆË©¶È®ì„ÅßÂæó„Çâ„Çå„ÅüÊÉÖÂ†±„ÅØ„ÄÅÁîü‰ΩìÊ®°ÂÄ£„ÅÆ‰∫åÁõ∏ÊÄß„Çπ„Ç≥„Éº„É´„Éâ„ÅÆË®≠Ë®à„Å´‰ΩøÁî®„Åï„Çå„Åü„ÄÇTCP„Çπ„Ç´„Ç™„Éº„É´„Éâ„ÅÆÂ≠îÂæÑ„ÅØ700-900m„ÄÅÊ∞óÂ≠îÁéá„ÅØ50-65%„Åß„ÅÇ„Çä„ÄÅÂúßÁ∏ÆÂº∑Â∫¶„ÅØ12MPa„Åß„ÅÇ„Å£„Åü„ÄÇËªüÈ™®Áõ∏„Å®„Çª„É©„Éü„ÉÉ„ÇØÁõ∏„ÅÆÈñì„ÅÆÈÅ©Âàá„Å™ÁµêÂêàÂäõ„ÇíÂæó„Çã„Åü„ÇÅ„Å´„ÄÅ„Éê„Ç§„Ç™„Éü„É°„ÉÜ„Ç£„ÉÉ„ÇØ„Å™ÁßªË°åÊßãÈÄ†„Å´„Çà„Å£„Å¶ÂΩ¢Êàê„Åï„Çå„ÅüÁâ©ÁêÜÁöÑ„É≠„ÉÉ„ÇØ„ÇíÁî®„ÅÑ„Åü„ÄÇ„Åù„ÅÆÁµêÊûú„ÄÅCSL„ÅØÂæìÊù•„ÅÆÊäÄË°ì„Å®ÊØîËºÉ„Åó„Å¶„ÄÅÈ™®ÁµÑÁπîÂ∑•Â≠¶„Å∏„ÅÆÂøúÁî®„Å´ÁêÜÊÉ≥ÁöÑ„Å™„Çπ„Ç´„Ç™„Éº„É´„Éâ„ÇíÂæó„Çã„Åü„ÇÅ„Å´ÂÑ™„Çå„ÅüÊÄßËÉΩ„ÇíÁô∫ÊèÆ„Åó„Åü„Å®ÁµêË´ñ‰ªò„Åë„Åü[91]„ÄÇMaterials „Éê„Ç§„Ç™„Ç¢„ÇØ„ÉÜ„Ç£„Éñ„Ç∞„É©„ÇπÔºàBGÔºâ„ÅØ„ÄÅÁÑ°Â≠îË≥™„Å®Âõ∫‰Ωì„ÅÆ‰∏°Êñπ„ÅÆÂΩ¢ÊÖã„ÅßÂ≠òÂú®„Åô„Çã„Éê„Ç§„Ç™„Çª„É©„Éü„ÉÉ„ÇØ„Çπ„ÅÆ‰∏ÄÁ®Æ„Åß„ÅÇ„Çã„ÄÇ„Éê„Ç§„Ç™„Ç¨„É©„Çπ„ÅØÔºå‰∫åÈÖ∏Âåñ„Ç±„Ç§Á¥†ÔºåÈÖ∏Âåñ„Éä„Éà„É™„Ç¶„É†ÔºåÈÖ∏Âåñ„Ç´„É´„Ç∑„Ç¶„É†Ôºå„É™„É≥„ÇíÂê´„ÇÄ„ÄÇ„Åì„Çå„Çâ„ÅÆÊàêÂàÜ„ÅÆÂê´ÊúâÁéá„ÇíÂ§â„Åà„Çã„Åì„Å®„Åß„ÄÅÊßò„ÄÖ„Å™„Çø„Ç§„Éó„ÅÆ„Éê„Ç§„Ç™„Ç¨„É©„Çπ„Åå‰Ωú„Çâ„Çå„Å¶„ÅÑ„Çã[94]„ÄÇ„Ç±„Ç§ÈÖ∏Â°©„ÅØ„ÄÅÁîü‰ΩìÊ¥ªÊÄß„Ç¨„É©„Çπ„ÅÆÁîü‰ΩìÈÅ©ÂêàÊÄß„Å´ÈáçË¶Å„Å™ÂΩπÂâ≤„ÇíÊûú„Åü„Åó„Å¶„ÅÑ„Çã„ÄÇ45ÔΩû52vol.%„ÅÆ„Ç±„Ç§ÈÖ∏Â°©„ÇíÂê´„ÇÄ„Éê„Ç§„Ç™„Ç¨„É©„Çπ„ÅØ„ÄÅÁêÜÊÉ≥ÁöÑ„Å™È™®-„Ç∞„É©„Éï„ÉàÁµêÂêà„ÇíÊúâ„Åô„Çã[95]„ÄÇ45S5-„Éê„Ç§„Ç™„Ç¨„É©„Çπ„ÅØ„ÄÅ„Çà„ÅèÁü•„Çâ„Çå„ÅüÂ∏ÇË≤©ÂìÅ„Åß„ÄÅÈ™®ÁΩÆÊèõ„Å´Â∫É„Åè‰ΩøÁî®„Åï„Çå„Å¶„ÅÑ„Çã[96]„ÄÇ„Åó„Åã„Åó„ÄÅ„Éê„Ç§„Ç™„Ç¨„É©„Çπ„ÅØ„ÄÅÊ©üÊ¢∞ÁöÑÁâπÊÄß„Åå‰Ωé„Åè„ÄÅËÑÜ„ÅÑ„Åü„ÇÅ„Å´ËÄêËç∑ÈáçÁî®ÈÄî„Å´„ÅØ‰∏çÂêë„Åç„Åß„ÅÇ„Çä„ÄÅÂÜÖÈÉ®ÁöÑ„Å´ËÑÜ„Åè„ÄÅÊ©üÊ¢∞ÁöÑÂº∑Â∫¶„Åå‰∏çË∂≥„Åó„Å¶„ÅÑ„Çã„Åü„ÇÅ„ÄÅ„ÅÑ„Åè„Å§„Åã„ÅÆÂà∂Èôê„ÇÇ„ÅÇ„Çã„ÄÇ„Åó„Åã„Åó„ÄÅ‰Ωï‰∫∫„Åã„ÅÆÁ†îÁ©∂ËÄÖ„ÅØ„ÄÅÊ©üÊ¢∞ÁöÑÁâπÊÄß„ÇíÈ´ò„ÇÅ„Çã„Åü„ÇÅ„Å´„ÄÅÈáëÂ±û„ÄÅ„Éù„É™„Éû„Éº„ÄÅ„Çª„É©„Éü„ÉÉ„ÇØ„Å™„Å©„ÅÆÊßò„ÄÖ„Å™Ê∑ªÂä†Áâ©„ÇíÂ†±Âëä„Åó„Å¶„ÅÑ„Çã[97-101]„ÄÇ
